<!DOCTYPE html><html lang="en"><head><meta charSet="utf-8"/><title>Threat Actors use Google Ads to Deploy VIDAR Stealer | Kroll</title><meta name="viewport" content="width=device-width, initial-scale=1"/><link rel="icon" href="https://www.kroll.com/favicon.ico"/><link rel="canonical" href="https://www.kroll.com/en/publications/cyber/threat-actors-google-ads-deploy-vidar-stealer"/><meta name="Title" content="Threat Actors use Google Ads to Deploy VIDAR Stealer | Kroll"/><meta name="description" content="Kroll has observed threat actors abusing Google Ads to deploy malware masquerading as legitimate downloads or software that has been “cracked” or modified to remove or disable features such as copy protection or adware. Read more."/><meta name="TemplateName" content="article page"/><meta name="RelatedRegions" content="49156f6a-dfa2-4da8-bacc-d7fecbb91155"/><meta name="RelatedRegionsDetails" content="North America"/><meta name="keywords" content="AvosLocker, AvosLocker Ransomware, Backup Targeting, Defense Evasion Techniques"/><meta name="ItemPath" content="/Publications/Cyber"/><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary"/><meta name="twitter:site" content="@krollwire"/><meta name="twitter:creator" content="@krollwire"/><meta name="twitter:title" content="Kroll has observed threat actors abusing Google Ads to deploy malware masquerading as legitimate downloads or software that has been “cracked” or modified to remove or disable features such as copy protection or adware. Read more."/><meta name="twitter:description" content="Kroll has observed threat actors abusing Google Ads to deploy malware masquerading as legitimate downloads or software that has been “cracked” or modified to remove or disable features such as copy protection or adware. Read more."/><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Thumbnail/google-ads-deploy-vidar-stealar.png?h=180&amp;iar=0&amp;w=160"/><meta name="google-site-verification" content="&lt;p&gt;Kroll has observed threat actors abusing Google Ads to deploy malware masquerading as legitimate downloads or software that has been &amp;ldquo;cracked&amp;rdquo; or modified to remove or disable features such as copy protection or adware. As part of our analysis of this trend and threat, we have identified specifically that &lt;a href=&quot;https://www.kroll.com/en/insights/publications/cyber/malware-analysis-vidar-version&quot;&gt;VIDAR malware&lt;/a&gt;, an information-stealing trojan, is using Google Ads to advertise spoofed domains and redirect users to fraudulent sites or malware downloads. Kroll is currently tracking the use of this tactic by ransomware groups globally, particularly groups that are assessed with medium confidence to be associated with former Conti ransomware affiliates such as Royal, Black Basta, and Hive ransomware operators. While the infection vector is the same, Zloader is typically used to deploy further malicious tooling to gain a foothold within the network during the &lt;a href=&quot;https://www.kroll.com/en/insights/publications/cyber/kroll-intrusion-lifecycle&quot;&gt;Intrusion Lifecycle&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;As an example of Kroll&amp;rsquo;s findings and analysis into this trend, we discovered a particular Google Ad that, while displaying the legitimate domain of the opensource image editing product GIMP, ultimately redirected the user to a typo-squatted domain, hosting a cloned website containing malicious downloads. This is particularly interesting as the format of the advert is controlled through Google&amp;rsquo;s Ad framework. The display domain highlighted below is extracted by Google from the target URL provided by the advertiser.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;
&lt;img src=&quot;https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/1.svg?h=574&amp;amp;w=1139&quot; style=&quot;margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;&quot; alt=&quot;Screenshot of Malicious Ad&quot; /&gt;
&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p style=&quot;text-align: center;&quot;&gt;Figure 1: Screenshot of Malicious Ad (Source: Kroll)&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;
&lt;img src=&quot;https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/2.svg?h=502&amp;amp;w=1139&quot; style=&quot;margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;&quot; alt=&quot;Screenshot of the Typo-Squatted Domain &quot; /&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p style=&quot;text-align: center;&quot;&gt;Figure 2: Screenshot of the Typo-Squatted Domain (Source: Kroll)&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;The malicious domain gilimp[.]org appears to have been registered on October 17, 2022, indicating that this advert could have been live for up to 16 days at the time of our analysis.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;
&lt;img src=&quot;https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/3.svg?h=902&amp;amp;w=1139&quot; style=&quot;margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;&quot; alt=&quot;Whois Record for Typo-Squatted Domain &quot; /&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p style=&quot;text-align: center;&quot;&gt;Figure 3: Whois Record for Typo-Squatted Domain (Source: Kroll)&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;At the time of the investigation, it was no longer possible to access the advertisement (&amp;ldquo;advert&amp;rdquo;), and the screenshots available online no longer showed the destination URL of the advert when hovered over to see the first step in the request chain, making it more difficult for Kroll to definitively determine exactly how the threat actor achieved this.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Kroll analyzed a binary on the malicious domain that was presented to appear as the GIMP software. The analysis showed that it was in fact VIDAR malware. Our experts were able to determine that the malware was stealing browser cookies and passwords, along with detailed system information, before sending these to a C2 IP address.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;The IP information for this IP address shows its geolocation as St. Petersburg in the Russian Federation.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;
&lt;img src=&quot;https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/4.svg?h=992&amp;amp;w=1139&quot; style=&quot;margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;&quot; alt=&quot;C2 IP Address Information &quot; /&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p style=&quot;text-align: center;&quot;&gt;Figure 4: C2 IP Address Information (Source: Kroll)&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;
&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;h2 style=&quot;font-size: 24px;&quot;&gt;Most Likely Methodologies&lt;/h2&gt;
&lt;p&gt;The Kroll Cyber Threat Intelligence team tested a number of theories leveraging the Google Ad workflow for how &amp;ldquo;malvertising&amp;rdquo; could lead to the deployment of the VIDAR Stealer. Kroll proposed with high confidence the below two most likely scenarios based on completed research to date:&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;ol style=&quot;margin-left: 40px;&quot;&gt;
    &lt;li&gt;A homoglyph attack utilizing international domain name scheme&lt;/li&gt;
    &lt;li&gt;Via manipulation of the tracking template URL option&amp;nbsp;&lt;/li&gt;
&lt;/ol&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;h3 style=&quot;font-size: 20px;&quot;&gt;Homoglyph Attack&amp;nbsp;&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;This attack method is documented by others within the Security and Incident Response communities and seems to be a favorite hypothesis shared by many to include Kroll&amp;rsquo;s researchers.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;
&lt;img src=&quot;https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/5.svg?h=495&amp;amp;w=1139&quot; style=&quot;margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;&quot; alt=&quot;Screenshot Showing the Setting Up of Homoglyph Attack in Google Ads &quot; /&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p style=&quot;text-align: center;&quot;&gt;Figure 5: Screenshot Showing the Setting Up of Homoglyph Attack in Google Ads (Source: Kroll)&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;As detailed in Figure 5, Kroll&amp;rsquo;s threat intelligence team would be able to set up an advert utilizing an international domain name that would pass most viewer&amp;rsquo;s initial inspection of the domain. If a homoglyph attack was used, it is an exceptionally effective approach with no obviously out-of-place characters.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;
&lt;img src=&quot;https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/6.svg?h=207&amp;amp;w=1139&quot; style=&quot;margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;&quot; alt=&quot;Zoomed In View of Ad &quot; /&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p style=&quot;text-align: center;&quot;&gt;Figure 6: Zoomed In View of Ad (Source: Kroll)&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Kroll observed that the ultimate page reached by clicking on the link was not an international domain, but a second, different typo-squatting domain. This inconsistency makes this method appear less probable since the threat actor would need to link to 2 domains via a redirect chain.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;However, there is a possibility that the threat actor did this to protect their homoglyph domain or they were aware that some web browsers will show the ascii format domain name in the address bar (for example: xn--gmp2ub[.]org instead of g&amp;iuml;mp[.]org), making the website appear more suspicious. Kroll&#x27;s testing of this process also identified Google&#x27;s automated domain checking processes which would normally frustrate a threat actor&#x27;s usage of this methodology.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;
&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;h3 style=&quot;font-size: 20px;&quot;&gt;Tracking Template URL&amp;nbsp;&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Google Ads allow for the use of a tracking link that would be the first link connected too, in order to store various parameters for your advertising campaign before forwarding on to the target page. With this tracking link set, the display domain remains the domain of the target URL.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src=&quot;https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/7.svg?h=473&amp;amp;w=1139&quot; style=&quot;margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;&quot; alt=&quot;Screenshot Showing the Setting Up of a Cross domain Tracking Template in Google Ads&quot; /&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p style=&quot;text-align: center;&quot;&gt;Figure 7: Screenshot Showing the Setting Up of a Cross domain Tracking Template in Google Ads (Source: Kroll)&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;It is possible that, by using a malicious tracking link, a threat actor could set up an advert for the legitimate gimp.org and redirect to their malicious page instead of the real page. This is currently the method Kroll assesses has been leveraged by Threat Actors in prior &lt;a href=&quot;https://www.kroll.com/en/insights/publications/cyber/kroll-intrusion-lifecycle&quot;&gt;Intrusion Lifecycles&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Kroll tested this methodology using a malicious tracking template hosted on a separate domain and successfully redirected an advert click to a third domain - the video of which can be viewed &lt;a rel=&quot;noopener noreferrer&quot; href=&quot;https://vimeo.com/767273760/317429a75b&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;here&lt;/a&gt;. The setup used the process described in the official &lt;a rel=&quot;noopener noreferrer&quot; href=&quot;https://support.google.com/google-ads/answer/6273460?hl=en-GB&amp;amp;visit_id=638030047801552628-3602104702&amp;amp;ref_topic=6273775&amp;amp;rd=1&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;Google documentation&lt;/a&gt; for cross-domain redirects.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;
&lt;img src=&quot;https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/8.svg?h=346&amp;amp;w=1139&quot; style=&quot;margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;&quot; alt=&quot;Screenshot of Active Advert Setup using Cross domain Tracking Template &quot; /&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p style=&quot;text-align: center;&quot;&gt;Figure 8: Screenshot of Active Advert Setup using Cross domain Tracking Template (Source: Kroll)&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;By utilizing a custom PHP script on the tracking domain, we were then able to redirect traffic to a proof-of-concept domain instead of the legitimate website. There is some automated checking performed by Google to detect incorrect redirecting; however, this was circumvented with minimal effort. It is likely this automated checking is designed to detect mistakes rather than this specific methodology.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;
&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;h2 style=&quot;font-size: 24px;&quot;&gt;Additional Methodologies&amp;nbsp;&lt;/h2&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Our team also explored a series of less likely scenarios:&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;ol style=&quot;margin-left: 40px;&quot;&gt;
    &lt;li&gt;A configuration setting within the Google Ads system allowing the ability to specify a different target domain to display domain either legitimately allowed or via a bug&amp;nbsp;&lt;/li&gt;
    &lt;li&gt;Use of an open redirect on the gimp.org site&amp;nbsp;&lt;/li&gt;
    &lt;li&gt;A bug in URL validation processes allowing for manipulation of display&amp;nbsp;&lt;/li&gt;
&lt;/ol&gt;
&lt;p&gt;
&lt;/p&gt;
&amp;nbsp;
&lt;h3 style=&quot;font-size: 20px;&quot;&gt;Configuration Setting&amp;nbsp;&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;To date, our team have been unable to produce a combination of settings in the Google Ads interface that would allow a different display domain from the target domain.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;
&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;h3 style=&quot;font-size: 20px;&quot;&gt;Use of Open Redirect&amp;nbsp;&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;The Google Ads system extracts the domain it displays in the advert from the Final URL field. If an open redirect were present on the gimp.org website and being used as the Final URL, &amp;ldquo;gimp.org&amp;rdquo; would be displayed. It is not possible to test whether Google Ads would detect this without an open redirect vulnerability being present to use in the test.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;img src=&quot;https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/9.svg?h=670&amp;amp;w=1139&quot; style=&quot;margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;&quot; alt=&quot;Screenshot Showing Hypothetical Setup of Attack Using an Open Redirect &quot; /&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p style=&quot;text-align: center;&quot;&gt;Figure 9: Screenshot Showing Hypothetical Setup of Attack Using an Open Redirect (Source: Kroll)&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;For this to work, there would have to be an open redirect vulnerability on the gimp.org website; additionally, the aforementioned redirection validation checks performed by google would need bypassing.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;
&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;h3 style=&quot;font-size: 20px;&quot;&gt;Validation Bug&amp;nbsp;&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;It is conceivable that a bug in the validation of inputs might have allowed the manipulation of the advert and target domain. Our team tried a numerous strategies to see what resulted as controlled tests. All tests were caught by server-side validation. However, they were successful in changing the display of the preview advert to not reflect the target domain.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;
&lt;img src=&quot;https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/10.svg?h=568&amp;amp;w=1139&quot; style=&quot;margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;&quot; alt=&quot;Screenshot Showing an Attempt to Manipulate the URL to Display Incorrectly &quot; /&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p style=&quot;text-align: center;&quot;&gt;Figure 10: Screenshot Showing an Attempt to Manipulate the URL to Display Incorrectly (Source: Kroll)&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;
&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;h3 style=&quot;font-size: 20px;&quot;&gt;Google Review&amp;nbsp;&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;As mentioned earlier, Google has a review process for adverts. Changes to the advert, including changing domain or tracking link will take the advert offline and require further review.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;
&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;h3 style=&quot;font-size: 20px;&quot;&gt;Impact&amp;nbsp;&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Now that this attack has been documented on various websites, it is very likely that other actors will attempt this technique because it can effectively turn any website into a watering hole attack, conveniently placing their malicious website at the top of the Google Search results.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;While it is particularly dangerous for sites that provide software for download, it could easily be expanded for other purposes. For example, a clone of a login page of a legitimate domain could be hosted for credential harvesting.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;
&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;h3 style=&quot;font-size: 20px;&quot;&gt;Kroll Recommendations&amp;nbsp;&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;ul style=&quot;margin-left: 40px;&quot;&gt;
    &lt;li&gt;Inform and educate personnel of this current attack methodology, making them aware of the dangers of downloading executables from websites that have not been verified as legitimate, particularly in relation to executables found after clicking through any form of online advertising.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/li&gt;
    &lt;li&gt;Where possible, restrict staff from downloading executables, a feature of many web filtering capable proxy servers.&lt;/li&gt;
    &lt;li&gt;Require all installs to be performed by information technology staff from vetted binary repositories.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/li&gt;
    &lt;li&gt;Employ &lt;a href=&quot;https://www.kroll.com/en/services/cyber-risk/managed-security/kroll-responder/mdr-microsoft&quot;&gt;endpoint detection and response&lt;/a&gt; (EDR) and next-generation anti-virus (NGAV) tools across all endpoints within the environment.&lt;/li&gt;
&lt;/ul&gt;
&lt;p&gt;
&lt;/p&gt;

16.9542 12.3975L12.2575 17.0942C11.9182 17.4335 11.3631 17.4335 11.0238 17.0942C10.6845 16.7546 10.6845 16.1998 11.0238 15.8605L15.7202 11.1638H15.7205ZM21.2453 12.167L18.3614 15.0509C17.9434 15.4686 17.4745 7.98694C17.3693 7.98694 17.264 8.02628 17.1847 8.10554L14.3011 10.9891C14.221 11.069 14.1808 11.174 14.1799 11.279H14.1822C14.1822 11.3845 14.2219 11.4895 14.3011 11.5688L14.3867 11.6544L13.1531 12.8881L13.0674 12.8022C12.6477 12.3828 12.4383 11.8306 12.4383 11.279L12.4406 11.2764C12.4406 10.725 12.6498 10.1737 13.0674 9.75574L15.9513 6.87186C16.3707 6.45215 16.9229 6.24273 17.4743 6.24273C18.0256 6.24273 18.5781 6.45244 18.9975 6.87186L21.2456 9.11996C21.6636 9.53765 21.8727 10.0893 21.8727 10.6406L21.8753 10.6432C21.8753 11.1948 21.6656 11.7467 21.2459 12.1661L21.2453 12.167Z" fill="#14487F"></path></g><defs><clipPath id="clip0_6673_972"><rect width="28" height="28" fill="white"></rect></clipPath></defs></svg></button><button class="text-DarkestGray text-[3.5rem] flex items-center justify-center font-semibold ml-[1rem]" aria-label="crossIcon"><svg width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 15 15" fill="none" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg"><path d="M12.8536 2.85355C13.0488 2.65829 13.0488 2.34171 12.8536 2.14645C12.6583 1.95118 12.3417 1.95118 12.1464 2.14645L7.5 6.79289L2.85355 2.14645C2.65829 1.95118 2.34171 1.95118 2.14645 2.14645C1.95118 2.34171 1.95118 2.65829 2.14645 2.85355L6.79289 7.5L2.14645 12.1464C1.95118 12.3417 1.95118 12.6583 2.14645 12.8536C2.34171 13.0488 2.65829 13.0488 2.85355 12.8536L7.5 8.20711L12.1464 12.8536C12.3417 13.0488 12.6583 13.0488 12.8536 12.8536C13.0488 12.6583 13.0488 12.3417 12.8536 12.1464L8.20711 7.5L12.8536 2.85355Z" fill="currentColor" fill-rule="evenodd" clip-rule="evenodd"></path></svg></button></div></div></div></div></div><div class="col-xl-12"></div></div></div></div></div><div class="container"><div class="component column-splitter row "><div class="col-12 col-lg-8"><div class="row"><div class="component"><div class="component-content"><div class="container mx-auto py-[2rem] "><div class="container"><div class="row"><div class="container grid col-12 gap-[3.25rem]  undefined"><div class="flex flex-col gap-[2.5rem] false "></div></div><div class="col-12 font-light text-Black"><div class="rich-text"><p>Kroll has observed threat actors abusing Google Ads to deploy malware masquerading as legitimate downloads or software that has been &ldquo;cracked&rdquo; or modified to remove or disable features such as copy protection or adware. As part of our analysis of this trend and threat, we have identified specifically that <a href="https://www.kroll.com/en/insights/publications/cyber/malware-analysis-vidar-version">VIDAR malware</a>, an information-stealing trojan, is using Google Ads to advertise spoofed domains and redirect users to fraudulent sites or malware downloads. Kroll is currently tracking the use of this tactic by ransomware groups globally, particularly groups that are assessed with medium confidence to be associated with former Conti ransomware affiliates such as Royal, Black Basta, and Hive ransomware operators. While the infection vector is the same, Zloader is typically used to deploy further malicious tooling to gain a foothold within the network during the <a href="https://www.kroll.com/en/insights/publications/cyber/kroll-intrusion-lifecycle">Intrusion Lifecycle</a>.</p>
<p>As an example of Kroll&rsquo;s findings and analysis into this trend, we discovered a particular Google Ad that, while displaying the legitimate domain of the opensource image editing product GIMP, ultimately redirected the user to a typo-squatted domain, hosting a cloned website containing malicious downloads. This is particularly interesting as the format of the advert is controlled through Google&rsquo;s Ad framework. The display domain highlighted below is extracted by Google from the target URL provided by the advertiser.</p>
<p>
<img src="https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/1.svg" style="margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;" alt="Screenshot of Malicious Ad" />
</p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 1: Screenshot of Malicious Ad (Source: Kroll)&nbsp;</p>
<p>
<img src="https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/2.svg" style="margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;" alt="Screenshot of the Typo-Squatted Domain " /></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 2: Screenshot of the Typo-Squatted Domain (Source: Kroll)&nbsp;</p>
<p>The malicious domain gilimp[.]org appears to have been registered on October 17, 2022, indicating that this advert could have been live for up to 16 days at the time of our analysis.&nbsp;</p>
<p>
<img src="https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/3.svg" style="margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;" alt="Whois Record for Typo-Squatted Domain " /></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 3: Whois Record for Typo-Squatted Domain (Source: Kroll)&nbsp;</p>
<p>At the time of the investigation, it was no longer possible to access the advertisement (&ldquo;advert&rdquo;), and the screenshots available online no longer showed the destination URL of the advert when hovered over to see the first step in the request chain, making it more difficult for Kroll to definitively determine exactly how the threat actor achieved this.&nbsp;</p>
<p>Kroll analyzed a binary on the malicious domain that was presented to appear as the GIMP software. The analysis showed that it was in fact VIDAR malware. Our experts were able to determine that the malware was stealing browser cookies and passwords, along with detailed system information, before sending these to a C2 IP address.&nbsp;</p>
<p>The IP information for this IP address shows its geolocation as St. Petersburg in the Russian Federation.&nbsp;</p>
<p>
<img src="https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/4.svg" style="margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;" alt="C2 IP Address Information " /></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 4: C2 IP Address Information (Source: Kroll)&nbsp;</p>
<p>
</p>
<h2 style="font-size: 24px;">Most Likely Methodologies</h2>
<p>The Kroll Cyber Threat Intelligence team tested a number of theories leveraging the Google Ad workflow for how &ldquo;malvertising&rdquo; could lead to the deployment of the VIDAR Stealer. Kroll proposed with high confidence the below two most likely scenarios based on completed research to date:</p>
<ol style="margin-left: 40px;">
    <li>A homoglyph attack utilizing international domain name scheme</li>
    <li>Via manipulation of the tracking template URL option&nbsp;</li>
</ol>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<h3 style="font-size: 20px;">Homoglyph Attack&nbsp;</h3>
<p>This attack method is documented by others within the Security and Incident Response communities and seems to be a favorite hypothesis shared by many to include Kroll&rsquo;s researchers.&nbsp;</p>
<p>
<img src="https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/5.svg" style="margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;" alt="Screenshot Showing the Setting Up of Homoglyph Attack in Google Ads " /></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 5: Screenshot Showing the Setting Up of Homoglyph Attack in Google Ads (Source: Kroll)&nbsp;</p>
<p>As detailed in Figure 5, Kroll&rsquo;s threat intelligence team would be able to set up an advert utilizing an international domain name that would pass most viewer&rsquo;s initial inspection of the domain. If a homoglyph attack was used, it is an exceptionally effective approach with no obviously out-of-place characters.&nbsp;</p>
<p>
<img src="https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/6.svg" style="margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;" alt="Zoomed In View of Ad " /></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 6: Zoomed In View of Ad (Source: Kroll)&nbsp;</p>
<p>Kroll observed that the ultimate page reached by clicking on the link was not an international domain, but a second, different typo-squatting domain. This inconsistency makes this method appear less probable since the threat actor would need to link to 2 domains via a redirect chain.&nbsp;</p>
<p>However, there is a possibility that the threat actor did this to protect their homoglyph domain or they were aware that some web browsers will show the ascii format domain name in the address bar (for example: xn--gmp2ub[.]org instead of g&iuml;mp[.]org), making the website appear more suspicious. Kroll's testing of this process also identified Google's automated domain checking processes which would normally frustrate a threat actor's usage of this methodology.</p>
<p>
</p>
<h3 style="font-size: 20px;">Tracking Template URL&nbsp;</h3>
<p>Google Ads allow for the use of a tracking link that would be the first link connected too, in order to store various parameters for your advertising campaign before forwarding on to the target page. With this tracking link set, the display domain remains the domain of the target URL.&nbsp;</p>
<p><img src="https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/7.svg" style="margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;" alt="Screenshot Showing the Setting Up of a Cross domain Tracking Template in Google Ads" /></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 7: Screenshot Showing the Setting Up of a Cross domain Tracking Template in Google Ads (Source: Kroll)&nbsp;</p>
<p>It is possible that, by using a malicious tracking link, a threat actor could set up an advert for the legitimate gimp.org and redirect to their malicious page instead of the real page. This is currently the method Kroll assesses has been leveraged by Threat Actors in prior <a href="https://www.kroll.com/en/insights/publications/cyber/kroll-intrusion-lifecycle">Intrusion Lifecycles</a>.</p>
<p>Kroll tested this methodology using a malicious tracking template hosted on a separate domain and successfully redirected an advert click to a third domain - the video of which can be viewed <a rel="noopener noreferrer" href="https://vimeo.com/767273760/317429a75b" target="_blank">here</a>. The setup used the process described in the official <a rel="noopener noreferrer" href="https://support.google.com/google-ads/answer/6273460?hl=en-GB&amp;visit_id=638030047801552628-3602104702&amp;ref_topic=6273775&amp;rd=1" target="_blank">Google documentation</a> for cross-domain redirects.</p>
<p>
<img src="https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/8.svg" style="margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;" alt="Screenshot of Active Advert Setup using Cross domain Tracking Template " /></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 8: Screenshot of Active Advert Setup using Cross domain Tracking Template (Source: Kroll)&nbsp;</p>
<p>By utilizing a custom PHP script on the tracking domain, we were then able to redirect traffic to a proof-of-concept domain instead of the legitimate website. There is some automated checking performed by Google to detect incorrect redirecting; however, this was circumvented with minimal effort. It is likely this automated checking is designed to detect mistakes rather than this specific methodology.&nbsp;</p>
<p>
</p>
<h2 style="font-size: 24px;">Additional Methodologies&nbsp;</h2>
<p>Our team also explored a series of less likely scenarios:&nbsp;</p>
<ol style="margin-left: 40px;">
    <li>A configuration setting within the Google Ads system allowing the ability to specify a different target domain to display domain either legitimately allowed or via a bug&nbsp;</li>
    <li>Use of an open redirect on the gimp.org site&nbsp;</li>
    <li>A bug in URL validation processes allowing for manipulation of display&nbsp;</li>
</ol>
<p>
</p>
&nbsp;
<h3 style="font-size: 20px;">Configuration Setting&nbsp;</h3>
<p>To date, our team have been unable to produce a combination of settings in the Google Ads interface that would allow a different display domain from the target domain.&nbsp;</p>
<p>
</p>
<h3 style="font-size: 20px;">Use of Open Redirect&nbsp;</h3>
<p>The Google Ads system extracts the domain it displays in the advert from the Final URL field. If an open redirect were present on the gimp.org website and being used as the Final URL, &ldquo;gimp.org&rdquo; would be displayed. It is not possible to test whether Google Ads would detect this without an open redirect vulnerability being present to use in the test.&nbsp;</p>
<img src="https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/9.svg" style="margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;" alt="Screenshot Showing Hypothetical Setup of Attack Using an Open Redirect " />
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 9: Screenshot Showing Hypothetical Setup of Attack Using an Open Redirect (Source: Kroll)&nbsp;</p>
<p>For this to work, there would have to be an open redirect vulnerability on the gimp.org website; additionally, the aforementioned redirection validation checks performed by google would need bypassing.</p>
<p>
</p>
<h3 style="font-size: 20px;">Validation Bug&nbsp;</h3>
<p>It is conceivable that a bug in the validation of inputs might have allowed the manipulation of the advert and target domain. Our team tried a numerous strategies to see what resulted as controlled tests. All tests were caught by server-side validation. However, they were successful in changing the display of the preview advert to not reflect the target domain.&nbsp;</p>
<p>
<img src="https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/10.svg" style="margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;" alt="Screenshot Showing an Attempt to Manipulate the URL to Display Incorrectly " /></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 10: Screenshot Showing an Attempt to Manipulate the URL to Display Incorrectly (Source: Kroll)&nbsp;</p>
<p>
</p>
<h3 style="font-size: 20px;">Google Review&nbsp;</h3>
<p>As mentioned earlier, Google has a review process for adverts. Changes to the advert, including changing domain or tracking link will take the advert offline and require further review.</p>
<p>
</p>
<h3 style="font-size: 20px;">Impact&nbsp;</h3>
<p>Now that this attack has been documented on various websites, it is very likely that other actors will attempt this technique because it can effectively turn any website into a watering hole attack, conveniently placing their malicious website at the top of the Google Search results.</p>
<p>While it is particularly dangerous for sites that provide software for download, it could easily be expanded for other purposes. For example, a clone of a login page of a legitimate domain could be hosted for credential harvesting.</p>
<p>
</p>
<h3 style="font-size: 20px;">Kroll Recommendations&nbsp;</h3>
<ul style="margin-left: 40px;">
    <li>Inform and educate personnel of this current attack methodology, making them aware of the dangers of downloading executables from websites that have not been verified as legitimate, particularly in relation to executables found after clicking through any form of online advertising.&nbsp;</li>
    <li>Where possible, restrict staff from downloading executables, a feature of many web filtering capable proxy servers.</li>
    <li>Require all installs to be performed by information technology staff from vetted binary repositories.&nbsp;</li>
    <li>Employ <a href="https://www.kroll.com/en/services/cyber-risk/managed-security/kroll-responder/mdr-microsoft">endpoint detection and response</a> (EDR) and next-generation anti-virus (NGAV) tools across all endpoints within the environment.</li>
</ul>
<p>
</p>

"},"Summary Title":{"value":""},"Title":{"value":"Threat Exposure Management"}}}],"Service Title":{"value":""},"Related Regions":[{"id":"49156f6a-dfa2-4da8-bacc-d7fecbb91155","url":"/Data/Content/Locations/North-America","name":"North America","displayName":"North America","fields":{"PageID":{"value":"{8FDB2445-4DBA-4429-80F1-4D9A65EA8592}"},"TemplateID":{"value":"{A87A00B1-E6DB-45AB-8B54-636FEC3B5523}"},"TemplateName":{"value":"Folder"},"PageURL":{"value":{"querystring":"","id":"{8FDB2445-4DBA-4429-80F1-4D9A65EA8592}","target":"","linktype":"internal","text":"North America","url":"/Kroll/Kroll/Home/Global Locations/North America","href":"/Global-Locations/North-America"}},"Short Description":{"value":""},"Summary Title":{"value":""},"Title":{"value":"North America"}}}],"Category":{"value":"Threat Intelligence"},"Category Link":null,"Related Topics":[],"Related Industries":[],"Default Read Time":{"value":"7"},"Override Read Time":{"value":""},"Button Colour":null,"Hide See All People Link":{"value":false},"Related Insights":[],"Related Insights Title":{"value":""},"CSS Class":{"value":"ArticleDetail"},"Hero BG Check":{"value":false},"Is Contact Us":{"value":false},"Return to Top":{"value":true},"Add Ceros Script":{"value":false},"Body Tag":{"value":""},"Head Tag":{"value":""},"Facebook Description":{"value":""},"Facebook Image":{"value":{}},"Facebook Title":{"value":""},"Facebook Url":{"value":""},"Site Name":{"value":"Kroll"},"Author":{"value":""},"Canonical Url":{"value":""},"Exclude From Sitemap":{"value":false},"Meta Description":{"value":"Kroll has observed threat actors abusing Google Ads to deploy malware masquerading as legitimate downloads or software that has been “cracked” or modified to remove or disable features such as copy protection or adware. Read more."},"Meta Keywords":{"value":"AvosLocker, AvosLocker Ransomware, Backup Targeting, Defense Evasion Techniques"},"Meta Title":{"value":"Threat Actors use Google Ads to Deploy VIDAR Stealer | Kroll"},"Structured Details":{"value":""},"Twitter Card":{"value":"summary"},"Twitter Creator":{"value":"@krollwire"},"Twitter Description":{"value":""},"Twitter Image":{"value":{}},"Twitter Site":{"value":"@krollwire"},"Twitter Title":{"value":""},"Twitter URL":{"value":""},"NavigationClass":null,"NavigationFilter":[],"NavigationTitle":{"value":"$name"},"SxaTags":[],"Page Design":null,"Desktop Image":{"value":{"src":"https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Feature-Images/google-ads-deploy-vidar-stealar.png?h=200\u0026iar=0\u0026w=300","alt":"Threat Actors use Google Ads to Deploy VIDAR Stealer","width":"300","height":"200","title":"Threat Actors use Google Ads to Deploy VIDAR Stealer"}},"Image Caption":{"value":""},"Is Background Image":{"value":false},"Mobile Image":{"value":{}},"Call to Action":{"value":{"href":""}},"Desktop Video":{"value":null},"Mobile Video":{"value":null},"Video Caption":{"value":""},"Youtube Video Caption":{"value":""},"Youtube Video ID":{"value":""},"Date":{"value":"2022-12-13T00:00:00Z"},"Background Colour":null,"Is Light Text":{"value":false},"Title":{"value":"Threat Actors use Google Ads to Deploy VIDAR Stealer"},"Description":{"value":"\u003cp\u003eKroll has observed threat actors abusing Google Ads to deploy malware masquerading as legitimate downloads or software that has been \u0026ldquo;cracked\u0026rdquo; or modified to remove or disable features such as copy protection or adware. As part of our analysis of this trend and threat, we have identified specifically that \u003ca href=\"https://www.kroll.com/en/insights/publications/cyber/malware-analysis-vidar-version\"\u003eVIDAR malware\u003c/a\u003e, an information-stealing trojan, is using Google Ads to advertise spoofed domains and redirect users to fraudulent sites or malware downloads. Kroll is currently tracking the use of this tactic by ransomware groups globally, particularly groups that are assessed with medium confidence to be associated with former Conti ransomware affiliates such as Royal, Black Basta, and Hive ransomware operators. While the infection vector is the same, Zloader is typically used to deploy further malicious tooling to gain a foothold within the network during the \u003ca href=\"https://www.kroll.com/en/insights/publications/cyber/kroll-intrusion-lifecycle\"\u003eIntrusion Lifecycle\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAs an example of Kroll\u0026rsquo;s findings and analysis into this trend, we discovered a particular Google Ad that, while displaying the legitimate domain of the opensource image editing product GIMP, ultimately redirected the user to a typo-squatted domain, hosting a cloned website containing malicious downloads. This is particularly interesting as the format of the advert is controlled through Google\u0026rsquo;s Ad framework. The display domain highlighted below is extracted by Google from the target URL provided by the advertiser.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003cimg src=\"https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/1.svg?h=574\u0026amp;w=1139\" style=\"margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;\" alt=\"Screenshot of Malicious Ad\" /\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 1: Screenshot of Malicious Ad (Source: Kroll)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003cimg src=\"https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/2.svg?h=502\u0026amp;w=1139\" style=\"margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;\" alt=\"Screenshot of the Typo-Squatted Domain \" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 2: Screenshot of the Typo-Squatted Domain (Source: Kroll)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe malicious domain gilimp[.]org appears to have been registered on October 17, 2022, indicating that this advert could have been live for up to 16 days at the time of our analysis.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003cimg src=\"https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/3.svg?h=902\u0026amp;w=1139\" style=\"margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;\" alt=\"Whois Record for Typo-Squatted Domain \" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 3: Whois Record for Typo-Squatted Domain (Source: Kroll)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAt the time of the investigation, it was no longer possible to access the advertisement (\u0026ldquo;advert\u0026rdquo;), and the screenshots available online no longer showed the destination URL of the advert when hovered over to see the first step in the request chain, making it more difficult for Kroll to definitively determine exactly how the threat actor achieved this.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eKroll analyzed a binary on the malicious domain that was presented to appear as the GIMP software. The analysis showed that it was in fact VIDAR malware. Our experts were able to determine that the malware was stealing browser cookies and passwords, along with detailed system information, before sending these to a C2 IP address.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe IP information for this IP address shows its geolocation as St. Petersburg in the Russian Federation.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003cimg src=\"https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/4.svg?h=992\u0026amp;w=1139\" style=\"margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;\" alt=\"C2 IP Address Information \" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 4: C2 IP Address Information (Source: Kroll)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2 style=\"font-size: 24px;\"\u003eMost Likely Methodologies\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe Kroll Cyber Threat Intelligence team tested a number of theories leveraging the Google Ad workflow for how \u0026ldquo;malvertising\u0026rdquo; could lead to the deployment of the VIDAR Stealer. Kroll proposed with high confidence the below two most likely scenarios based on completed research to date:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003col style=\"margin-left: 40px;\"\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eA homoglyph attack utilizing international domain name scheme\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eVia manipulation of the tracking template URL option\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ol\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 style=\"font-size: 20px;\"\u003eHomoglyph Attack\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis attack method is documented by others within the Security and Incident Response communities and seems to be a favorite hypothesis shared by many to include Kroll\u0026rsquo;s researchers.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003cimg src=\"https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/5.svg?h=495\u0026amp;w=1139\" style=\"margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;\" alt=\"Screenshot Showing the Setting Up of Homoglyph Attack in Google Ads \" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 5: Screenshot Showing the Setting Up of Homoglyph Attack in Google Ads (Source: Kroll)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAs detailed in Figure 5, Kroll\u0026rsquo;s threat intelligence team would be able to set up an advert utilizing an international domain name that would pass most viewer\u0026rsquo;s initial inspection of the domain. If a homoglyph attack was used, it is an exceptionally effective approach with no obviously out-of-place characters.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003cimg src=\"https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/6.svg?h=207\u0026amp;w=1139\" style=\"margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;\" alt=\"Zoomed In View of Ad \" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 6: Zoomed In View of Ad (Source: Kroll)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eKroll observed that the ultimate page reached by clicking on the link was not an international domain, but a second, different typo-squatting domain. This inconsistency makes this method appear less probable since the threat actor would need to link to 2 domains via a redirect chain.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eHowever, there is a possibility that the threat actor did this to protect their homoglyph domain or they were aware that some web browsers will show the ascii format domain name in the address bar (for example: xn--gmp2ub[.]org instead of g\u0026iuml;mp[.]org), making the website appear more suspicious. Kroll's testing of this process also identified Google's automated domain checking processes which would normally frustrate a threat actor's usage of this methodology.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 style=\"font-size: 20px;\"\u003eTracking Template URL\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eGoogle Ads allow for the use of a tracking link that would be the first link connected too, in order to store various parameters for your advertising campaign before forwarding on to the target page. With this tracking link set, the display domain remains the domain of the target URL.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/7.svg?h=473\u0026amp;w=1139\" style=\"margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;\" alt=\"Screenshot Showing the Setting Up of a Cross domain Tracking Template in Google Ads\" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 7: Screenshot Showing the Setting Up of a Cross domain Tracking Template in Google Ads (Source: Kroll)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIt is possible that, by using a malicious tracking link, a threat actor could set up an advert for the legitimate gimp.org and redirect to their malicious page instead of the real page. This is currently the method Kroll assesses has been leveraged by Threat Actors in prior \u003ca href=\"https://www.kroll.com/en/insights/publications/cyber/kroll-intrusion-lifecycle\"\u003eIntrusion Lifecycles\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eKroll tested this methodology using a malicious tracking template hosted on a separate domain and successfully redirected an advert click to a third domain - the video of which can be viewed \u003ca rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://vimeo.com/767273760/317429a75b\" target=\"_blank\"\u003ehere\u003c/a\u003e. The setup used the process described in the official \u003ca rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://support.google.com/google-ads/answer/6273460?hl=en-GB\u0026amp;visit_id=638030047801552628-3602104702\u0026amp;ref_topic=6273775\u0026amp;rd=1\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eGoogle documentation\u003c/a\u003e for cross-domain redirects.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003cimg src=\"https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/8.svg?h=346\u0026amp;w=1139\" style=\"margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;\" alt=\"Screenshot of Active Advert Setup using Cross domain Tracking Template \" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 8: Screenshot of Active Advert Setup using Cross domain Tracking Template (Source: Kroll)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBy utilizing a custom PHP script on the tracking domain, we were then able to redirect traffic to a proof-of-concept domain instead of the legitimate website. There is some automated checking performed by Google to detect incorrect redirecting; however, this was circumvented with minimal effort. It is likely this automated checking is designed to detect mistakes rather than this specific methodology.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2 style=\"font-size: 24px;\"\u003eAdditional Methodologies\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOur team also explored a series of less likely scenarios:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003col style=\"margin-left: 40px;\"\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eA configuration setting within the Google Ads system allowing the ability to specify a different target domain to display domain either legitimately allowed or via a bug\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eUse of an open redirect on the gimp.org site\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eA bug in URL validation processes allowing for manipulation of display\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ol\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u0026nbsp;\n\u003ch3 style=\"font-size: 20px;\"\u003eConfiguration Setting\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eTo date, our team have been unable to produce a combination of settings in the Google Ads interface that would allow a different display domain from the target domain.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 style=\"font-size: 20px;\"\u003eUse of Open Redirect\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe Google Ads system extracts the domain it displays in the advert from the Final URL field. If an open redirect were present on the gimp.org website and being used as the Final URL, \u0026ldquo;gimp.org\u0026rdquo; would be displayed. It is not possible to test whether Google Ads would detect this without an open redirect vulnerability being present to use in the test.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cimg src=\"https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/9.svg?h=670\u0026amp;w=1139\" style=\"margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;\" alt=\"Screenshot Showing Hypothetical Setup of Attack Using an Open Redirect \" /\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 9: Screenshot Showing Hypothetical Setup of Attack Using an Open Redirect (Source: Kroll)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFor this to work, there would have to be an open redirect vulnerability on the gimp.org website; additionally, the aforementioned redirection validation checks performed by google would need bypassing.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 style=\"font-size: 20px;\"\u003eValidation Bug\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIt is conceivable that a bug in the validation of inputs might have allowed the manipulation of the advert and target domain. Our team tried a numerous strategies to see what resulted as controlled tests. All tests were caught by server-side validation. However, they were successful in changing the display of the preview advert to not reflect the target domain.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003cimg src=\"https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/10.svg?h=568\u0026amp;w=1139\" style=\"margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;\" alt=\"Screenshot Showing an Attempt to Manipulate the URL to Display Incorrectly \" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 10: Screenshot Showing an Attempt to Manipulate the URL to Display Incorrectly (Source: Kroll)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 style=\"font-size: 20px;\"\u003eGoogle Review\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAs mentioned earlier, Google has a review process for adverts. Changes to the advert, including changing domain or tracking link will take the advert offline and require further review.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 style=\"font-size: 20px;\"\u003eImpact\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eNow that this attack has been documented on various websites, it is very likely that other actors will attempt this technique because it can effectively turn any website into a watering hole attack, conveniently placing their malicious website at the top of the Google Search results.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhile it is particularly dangerous for sites that provide software for download, it could easily be expanded for other purposes. For example, a clone of a login page of a legitimate domain could be hosted for credential harvesting.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 style=\"font-size: 20px;\"\u003eKroll Recommendations\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cul style=\"margin-left: 40px;\"\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eInform and educate personnel of this current attack methodology, making them aware of the dangers of downloading executables from websites that have not been verified as legitimate, particularly in relation to executables found after clicking through any form of online advertising.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eWhere possible, restrict staff from downloading executables, a feature of many web filtering capable proxy servers.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eRequire all installs to be performed by information technology staff from vetted binary repositories.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eEmploy \u003ca href=\"https://www.kroll.com/en/services/cyber-risk/managed-security/kroll-responder/mdr-microsoft\"\u003eendpoint detection and response\u003c/a\u003e (EDR) and next-generation anti-virus (NGAV) tools across all endpoints within the environment.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eLearn more about \u003ca His team of elite analysts, supported by advanced technology, help refine Kroll\u0026rsquo;s analytical and investigative offerings firmwide. The CTI team regularly enhances cyber investigations by providing in-depth analysis on attack methods and actor groups, research on evolving indicators of compromise, and locating compromised data and/or credentials. Keith\u0026rsquo;s CTI team also develops statistical and behavioral models for predictive purposes as well as macro-environmental analyses.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn addition, Keith supervises multidisciplinary teams investigating cybercrime and data loss investigations, including but not limited to ransomware and malware, business email compromise, unauthorized access to cloud-based environments (e.g., Office 365), and vulnerabilities originating in third-party software and services. He has additionally provided consulting support on hundreds of engagements involving diverse business and legal issues, including intellectual property theft, defensible PII-PHI deletion, accounting fraud, money laundering, employee misconduct and forensic application development.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eKeith joined the U.S. Secret Service in 2002, ultimately leading the USSS Cyber Intelligence Section, Criminal Investigation Division, where he managed the agency\u0026rsquo;s national response to attacks on the financial infrastructure of the U.S. that inflicted hundreds of millions of dollars of losses. In this role, he also coordinated complex international investigations that targeted transnational organized crime networks with an emphasis on cyber and information security. Under Keith\u0026rsquo;s leadership, a number of these cases resulted in the apprehension of highly sophisticated cybercriminals who collectively were responsible for causing over $1 billion in financial losses.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFrom 2012-2016, Keith was an integral member of protection details for the President, Vice President, Homeland Security Advisor to Terrorism and Presidential Chief of Staff. Earlier, from 2012-2015, Keith was with the Counter Assault Team in the Special Operations Division, which often involved coordinating security in varying international environments for the USSS protective mission and incorporated multiple U.S. federal agencies, local law enforcement and foreign government personnel.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eEarlier in his career, Keith led several domestic and global investigations working with the U.S. Department of Justice Computer Crime and Intellectual Property Section (CCIPS) and Office of International Affairs (OIA). From 2004-2010, Keith managed the Electronic Crimes Task Force in support of all electronic crime investigations, computer forensic examinations and cell phone data extraction in the Louisville Field Office District. In this role, he also established the operation of a forensic laboratory; provided expert testimony in multiple state and federal cases; and coordinated training for USSS agents, local law enforcement and industry representatives.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eA noted authority on cyber and intelligence-related topics, Keith has served as a certified expert witness in court proceedings and is frequently asked to speak at public and private conferences, webinars and panel discussions.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch5\u003eRepresentative Cyber Threat Intelligence (CTI) Matters\u003c/h5\u003e\n\u003cul style=\"margin-left: 40px;\"\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eA client\u0026rsquo;s philanthropic day of action was threatened by a group of threat actors planning to hijack their social media campaign to spread messages of hate. Kroll CTI analysts conducted regular monitoring to identify negative activity and performed covert analysis to infiltrate the threat actor group and discover their operational plans. Kroll shared this information with the client and their counsel, which led to swift action by third-party providers to thwart the planned attack. \u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eA client\u0026rsquo;s network was targeted with ransomware, and the attackers claimed they were going to post the client\u0026rsquo;s data on an actor-controlled website. Kroll\u0026rsquo;s CTI dark web experts were able to actively monitor the website in order to determine if files had actually been obtained and alert the client as to when and how many files had been published. Additionally, the CTI team was able to provide information on the attacker\u0026rsquo;s history of victims, ransom amount, and tactics, techniques and procedures related to the ransomware Keith\u0026rsquo;s role as leader of the Cyber Threat Intelligence (CTI) program, he conceptualizes strategies and methodologies on how to translate diverse information sources, including social media and dark web channels, into intelligence that can be used to help clients more fully assess threats, vulnerabilities and trends. His team of elite analysts, supported by advanced technology, help refine Kroll\u0026rsquo;s analytical and investigative offerings firmwide. The CTI team regularly enhances cyber investigations by providing in-depth analysis on attack methods and actor groups, research on evolving indicators of compromise, and locating compromised data and/or credentials. Keith\u0026rsquo;s CTI team also develops statistical and behavioral models for predictive purposes as well as macro-environmental analyses.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn addition, Keith supervises multidisciplinary teams investigating cybercrime and data loss investigations, including but not limited to ransomware and malware, business email compromise, unauthorized access to cloud-based environments (e.g., Office 365), and vulnerabilities originating in third-party software and services. He has additionally provided consulting support on hundreds of engagements involving diverse business and legal issues, including intellectual property theft, defensible PII-PHI deletion, accounting fraud, money laundering, employee misconduct and forensic application development.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eKeith joined the U.S. Secret Service in 2002, ultimately leading the USSS Cyber Intelligence Section, Criminal Investigation Division, where he managed the agency\u0026rsquo;s national response to attacks on the financial infrastructure of the U.S. that inflicted hundreds of millions of dollars of losses. In this role, he also coordinated complex international investigations that targeted transnational organized crime networks with an emphasis on cyber and information security. Under Keith\u0026rsquo;s leadership, a number of these cases resulted in the apprehension of highly sophisticated cybercriminals who collectively were responsible for causing over $1 billion in financial losses.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFrom 2012-2016, Keith was an integral member of protection details for the President, Vice President, Homeland Security Advisor to Terrorism and Presidential Chief of Staff. col-lg-8","ColumnWidth2":"col-12 col-lg-4","DynamicPlaceholderId":"23","EnabledPlaceholders":"1,2"},"placeholders":{"column-1-{*}":[{"uid":"a81e9e40-549c-4ffc-817e-ee40867028e3","componentName":"ArticleDetail","dataSource":"","fields":{"data":{"contextItem":{"image":{"jsonValue":{"value":{}}},"cta":{"jsonValue":{"value":{"href":""}},"targetItem":null},"shortDescription":{"jsonValue":{"value":""}},"description":{"jsonValue":{"value":"\u003cp\u003eKroll has observed threat actors abusing Google Ads to deploy malware masquerading as legitimate downloads or software that has been \u0026ldquo;cracked\u0026rdquo; or modified to remove or disable features such as copy protection or adware. As part of our analysis of this trend and threat, we have identified specifically that \u003ca href=\"https://www.kroll.com/en/insights/publications/cyber/malware-analysis-vidar-version\"\u003eVIDAR malware\u003c/a\u003e, an information-stealing trojan, is using Google Ads to advertise spoofed domains and redirect users to fraudulent sites or malware downloads. Kroll is currently tracking the use of this tactic by ransomware groups globally, particularly groups that are assessed with medium confidence to be associated with former Conti ransomware affiliates such as Royal, Black Basta, and Hive ransomware operators. While the infection vector is the same, Zloader is typically used to deploy further malicious tooling to gain a foothold within the network during the \u003ca href=\"https://www.kroll.com/en/insights/publications/cyber/kroll-intrusion-lifecycle\"\u003eIntrusion Lifecycle\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAs an example of Kroll\u0026rsquo;s findings and analysis into this trend, we discovered a particular Google Ad that, while displaying the legitimate domain of the opensource image editing product GIMP, ultimately redirected the user to a typo-squatted domain, hosting a cloned website containing malicious downloads. This is particularly interesting as the format of the advert is controlled through Google\u0026rsquo;s Ad framework. The display domain highlighted below is extracted by Google from the target URL provided by the advertiser.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003cimg src=\"https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/1.svg\" style=\"margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;\" alt=\"Screenshot of Malicious Ad\" /\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 1: Screenshot of Malicious Ad (Source: Kroll)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003cimg src=\"https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/2.svg\" style=\"margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;\" alt=\"Screenshot of the Typo-Squatted Domain \" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 2: Screenshot of the Typo-Squatted Domain (Source: Kroll)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe malicious domain gilimp[.]org appears to have been registered on October 17, 2022, indicating that this advert could have been live for up to 16 days at the time of our analysis.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003cimg src=\"https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/3.svg\" style=\"margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;\" alt=\"Whois Record for Typo-Squatted Domain \" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 3: Whois Record for Typo-Squatted Domain (Source: Kroll)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAt the time of the investigation, it was no longer possible to access the advertisement (\u0026ldquo;advert\u0026rdquo;), and the screenshots available online no longer showed the destination URL of the advert when hovered over to see the first step in the request chain, making it more difficult for Kroll to definitively determine exactly how the threat actor achieved this.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eKroll analyzed a binary on the malicious domain that was presented to appear as the GIMP software. The analysis showed that it was in fact VIDAR malware. Our experts were able to determine that the malware was stealing browser cookies and passwords, along with detailed system information, before sending these to a C2 IP address.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe IP information for this IP address shows its geolocation as St. Petersburg in the Russian Federation.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003cimg src=\"https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/4.svg\" style=\"margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;\" alt=\"C2 IP Address Information \" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 4: C2 IP Address Information (Source: Kroll)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2 style=\"font-size: 24px;\"\u003eMost Likely Methodologies\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe Kroll Cyber Threat Intelligence team tested a number of theories leveraging the Google Ad workflow for how \u0026ldquo;malvertising\u0026rdquo; could lead to the deployment of the VIDAR Stealer. Kroll proposed with high confidence the below two most likely scenarios based on completed research to date:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003col style=\"margin-left: 40px;\"\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eA homoglyph attack utilizing international domain name scheme\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eVia manipulation of the tracking template URL option\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ol\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 style=\"font-size: 20px;\"\u003eHomoglyph Attack\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis attack method is documented by others within the Security and Incident Response communities and seems to be a favorite hypothesis shared by many to include Kroll\u0026rsquo;s researchers.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003cimg src=\"https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/5.svg\" style=\"margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;\" alt=\"Screenshot Showing the Setting Up of Homoglyph Attack in Google Ads \" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 5: Screenshot Showing the Setting Up of Homoglyph Attack in Google Ads (Source: Kroll)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAs detailed in Figure 5, Kroll\u0026rsquo;s threat intelligence team would be able to set up an advert utilizing an international domain name that would pass most viewer\u0026rsquo;s initial inspection of the domain. If a homoglyph attack was used, it is an exceptionally effective approach with no obviously out-of-place characters.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003cimg src=\"https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/6.svg\" style=\"margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;\" alt=\"Zoomed In View of Ad \" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 6: Zoomed In View of Ad (Source: Kroll)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eKroll observed that the ultimate page reached by clicking on the link was not an international domain, but a second, different typo-squatting domain. This inconsistency makes this method appear less probable since the threat actor would need to link to 2 domains via a redirect chain.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eHowever, there is a possibility that the threat actor did this to protect their homoglyph domain or they were aware that some web browsers will show the ascii format domain name in the address bar (for example: xn--gmp2ub[.]org instead of g\u0026iuml;mp[.]org), making the website appear more suspicious. Kroll's testing of this process also identified Google's automated domain checking processes which would normally frustrate a threat actor's usage of this methodology.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 style=\"font-size: 20px;\"\u003eTracking Template URL\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eGoogle Ads allow for the use of a tracking link that would be the first link connected too, in order to store various parameters for your advertising campaign before forwarding on to the target page. With this tracking link set, the display domain remains the domain of the target URL.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/7.svg\" style=\"margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;\" alt=\"Screenshot Showing the Setting Up of a Cross domain Tracking Template in Google Ads\" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 7: Screenshot Showing the Setting Up of a Cross domain Tracking Template in Google Ads (Source: Kroll)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIt is possible that, by using a malicious tracking link, a threat actor could set up an advert for the legitimate gimp.org and redirect to their malicious page instead of the real page. This is currently the method Kroll assesses has been leveraged by Threat Actors in prior \u003ca href=\"https://www.kroll.com/en/insights/publications/cyber/kroll-intrusion-lifecycle\"\u003eIntrusion Lifecycles\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eKroll tested this methodology using a malicious tracking template hosted on a separate domain and successfully redirected an advert click to a third domain - the video of which can be viewed \u003ca rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://vimeo.com/767273760/317429a75b\" target=\"_blank\"\u003ehere\u003c/a\u003e. The setup used the process described in the official \u003ca rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://support.google.com/google-ads/answer/6273460?hl=en-GB\u0026amp;visit_id=638030047801552628-3602104702\u0026amp;ref_topic=6273775\u0026amp;rd=1\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eGoogle documentation\u003c/a\u003e for cross-domain redirects.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003cimg src=\"https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/8.svg\" style=\"margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;\" alt=\"Screenshot of Active Advert Setup using Cross domain Tracking Template \" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 8: Screenshot of Active Advert Setup using Cross domain Tracking Template (Source: Kroll)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBy utilizing a custom PHP script on the tracking domain, we were then able to redirect traffic to a proof-of-concept domain instead of the legitimate website. There is some automated checking performed by Google to detect incorrect redirecting; however, this was circumvented with minimal effort. It is likely this automated checking is designed to detect mistakes rather than this specific methodology.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2 style=\"font-size: 24px;\"\u003eAdditional Methodologies\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOur team also explored a series of less likely scenarios:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003col style=\"margin-left: 40px;\"\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eA configuration setting within the Google Ads system allowing the ability to specify a different target domain to display domain either legitimately allowed or via a bug\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eUse of an open redirect on the gimp.org site\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eA bug in URL validation processes allowing for manipulation of display\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ol\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u0026nbsp;\n\u003ch3 style=\"font-size: 20px;\"\u003eConfiguration Setting\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eTo date, our team have been unable to produce a combination of settings in the Google Ads interface that would allow a different display domain from the target domain.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 style=\"font-size: 20px;\"\u003eUse of Open Redirect\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe Google Ads system extracts the domain it displays in the advert from the Final URL field. If an open redirect were present on the gimp.org website and being used as the Final URL, \u0026ldquo;gimp.org\u0026rdquo; would be displayed. It is not possible to test whether Google Ads would detect this without an open redirect vulnerability being present to use in the test.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cimg src=\"https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/9.svg\" style=\"margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;\" alt=\"Screenshot Showing Hypothetical Setup of Attack Using an Open Redirect \" /\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 9: Screenshot Showing Hypothetical Setup of Attack Using an Open Redirect (Source: Kroll)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFor this to work, there would have to be an open redirect vulnerability on the gimp.org website; additionally, the aforementioned redirection validation checks performed by google would need bypassing.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 style=\"font-size: 20px;\"\u003eValidation Bug\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIt is conceivable that a bug in the validation of inputs might have allowed the manipulation of the advert and target domain. Our team tried a numerous strategies to see what resulted as controlled tests. All tests were caught by server-side validation. However, they were successful in changing the display of the preview advert to not reflect the target domain.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003cimg src=\"https://edge.sitecorecloud.io/krollllc17bf0-kroll6fee-proda464-0e9b/media/Kroll-Images/Insights/Google-Ads/10.svg\" style=\"margin-top: 0px; margin-bottom: 0px;\" alt=\"Screenshot Showing an Attempt to Manipulate the URL to Display Incorrectly \" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 10: Screenshot Showing an Attempt to Manipulate the URL to Display Incorrectly (Source: Kroll)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 style=\"font-size: 20px;\"\u003eGoogle Review\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAs mentioned earlier, Google has a review process for adverts. Changes to the advert, including changing domain or tracking link will take the advert offline and require further review.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 style=\"font-size: 20px;\"\u003eImpact\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eNow that this attack has been documented on various websites, it is very likely that other actors will attempt this technique because it can effectively turn any website into a watering hole attack, conveniently placing their malicious website at the top of the Google Search results.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhile it is particularly dangerous for sites that provide software for download, it could easily be expanded for other purposes. For example, a clone of a login page of a legitimate domain could be hosted for credential harvesting.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 style=\"font-size: 20px;\"\u003eKroll Recommendations\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cul style=\"margin-left: 40px;\"\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eInform and educate personnel of this current attack methodology, making them aware of the dangers of downloading executables from websites that have not been verified as legitimate, particularly in relation to executables found after clicking through any form of online advertising.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eWhere possible, restrict staff from downloading executables, a feature of many web filtering capable proxy servers.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eRequire all installs to be performed by information technology staff from vetted binary repositories.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eEmploy \u003ca href=\"https://www.kroll.com/en/services/cyber-risk/managed-security/kroll-responder/mdr-microsoft\"\u003eendpoint detection and response\u003c/a\u003e (EDR) and next-generation anti-virus (NGAV) tools across all endpoints within the environment.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eLearn more about \u003ca href=\"/en/services/cyber\"\u003eKroll\u0026rsquo;s end-to-end cyber security services\u003c/a\u003e\u0026nbsp;or call our \u003ca href=\"/en/contactus\"\u003eCyber Incident Response Hotline\u003c/a\u003e\u0026nbsp;to request immediate
